<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-level reference genome of the hornbeam, Carpinus fangiana"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-01-21"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Betulaceae, the birch family, comprises six living genera and over 160 species, many of which are economically valuable. To deepen our knowledge of Betulaceae species, we have sequenced the genome of a hornbeam, Carpinus fangiana, which belongs to the most species-rich genus of the Betulaceae subfamily Coryloideae. Based on over 75&#8201;Gb (~200x) of high-quality next-generation sequencing data, we assembled a 386.19&#8201;Mb&#8201;C. fangiana genome with contig N50 and scaffold N50 sizes of 35.32&#8201;kb and 1.91&#8201;Mb, respectively. Furthermore, 357.84&#8201;Mb of the genome was anchored to eight chromosomes using over 50&#8201;Gb (~130x) Hi-C sequencing data. Transcriptomes representing six tissues were sequenced to facilitate gene annotation, and over 5.50&#8201;Gb high-quality data were generated for each tissue. The structural annotation identified a total of 27,381 protein-coding genes in the assembled genome, of which 94.36% were functionally annotated. Additionally, 4,440 non-coding genes were predicted. 
                
                  
                    
                    
                    
                  
                
               Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.11382390"/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Betulaceae, the birch family, comprises six living genera and over 160 species, many of which are economically valuable. To deepen our knowledge of Betulaceae species, we have sequenced the genome of a hornbeam, <i>Carpinus fangiana</i>, which belongs to the most species-rich genus of the Betulaceae subfamily Coryloideae. Based on over 75 Gb (~200x) of high-quality next-generation sequencing data, we assembled a 386.19 Mb <i>C. fangiana</i> genome with contig N50 and scaffold N50 sizes of 35.32 kb and 1.91 Mb, respectively. Furthermore, 357.84 Mb of the genome was anchored to eight chromosomes using over 50 Gb (~130x) Hi-C sequencing data. Transcriptomes representing six tissues were sequenced to facilitate gene annotation, and over 5.50 Gb high-quality data were generated for each tissue. The structural annotation identified a total of 27,381 protein-coding genes in the assembled genome, of which 94.36% were functionally annotated. Additionally, 4,440 non-coding genes were predicted.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Measurement(s)
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          DNA • RNA • sequence_assembly • sequence feature annotation
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Technology Type(s)
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          DNA sequencing • RNA sequencing • genome assembly • sequence annotation
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Sample Characteristic - Organism
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Carpinus fangiana
                        </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>
              <p>Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: <a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.11382390">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.11382390</a></p></div></div></section>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Betulaceae, also known as the birch family, includes over 160 species of trees or shrubs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Christenhusz, M. J. M. &amp; Byng, J. W. The number of known plant species in the world and its annual increase. Phytotaxa. 261, 201–217 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d20126e492">1</a></sup>. It is divided into two subfamilies, Coryloideae and Betuloideae; Betuloideae comprises the genera <i>Alnus</i> and <i>Betula</i>, while Coryloideae comprises <i>Corylus</i>, <i>Ostryopsis</i>, <i>Carpinus</i> and <i>Ostrya</i>. These subfamilies and their genera are readily distinguished based on their different morphological characteristics, such as the samara of Coryloideae, the nuts of Betuloideae, and their different types of pollen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chen, Z. Phylogeny and phytogeography of the Betulaceae. Acta Phytotaxon. Sin. 32, 1–31 (1994)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d20126e515">2</a></sup>. In addition, cell biological investigations have revealed that Betulaceae species have very different chromosome numbers: the basic chromosome number is eight for <i>Carpinus</i>, <i>Ostrya</i>, <i>Ostryopsis</i> species, eleven for <i>Corylus</i> species, and fourteen for <i>Alnus</i> and <i>Betula</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Botta, R., Emanuel, E., Me, G., Sacerdote, S. &amp; Vallania, R. Indagine cariologica in alcune specie del genere Corylus (In Italian). Rivista di Ortoflorfrutticoltura Italiana. 70, 323–329 (1986)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d20126e538">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Snow, N. &amp; Goldblatt, P. Chromosome number in Ticodendron (Fagales, Ticodendraceae). Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 79, 906–907 (1992)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d20126e541">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Several Betulaceae species, notably those belonging to the genera <i>Betula</i>, <i>Alnus</i>, and <i>Carpinus</i>, are important components of forests in temperate regions, mountains, and subtropical areas, as well as important sources of timber and materials for traditional Chinese medicine. Some species of <i>Betula</i> and <i>Carpinus</i> are used as ornamental trees and widely planted in large parks and gardens. <i>Alnus</i> species can form symbioses with nitrogen-fixing bacteria of the genus <i>Frankia</i>, helping to enhance soil fertility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jin, W. Y., Cai, X. F., Na, M. K., Lee, J. J. &amp; Bae, K. H. Triterpenoids and Diarylheptainoids from Alnus hirsuta inhibit HIF-1 in AGS Cells. Arch. Pharm. Res. 30, 412–418 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20126e570">5</a></sup>. The fruits of <i>Corylus</i>, known as hazelnuts, are economically important. The birch family thus has remarkable ecological, economic, medicinal, and ornamental value. Additionally, Betulaceae is a relict family, and there are many reliable fossils of this family that have provided important paleobotanic insights<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Crane, P. R. &amp; Blackmore, S. Evolution, systematics, and fossil history of the Hamamelidae. (Published for the Systematics Association by the Clarendon Press, 1989)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d20126e577">6</a></sup>. However, only a few species of the family have been studied extensively in ways that could support their further development and utilization.</p><p>A few genomes of Betulaceae species have been published in recent years. The genomes of two Betuloideae members, <i>Betula pendula</i> (scaffold N50: 0.53 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Salojärvi, J. et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat. Genet. 49, 904–912 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d20126e587">7</a></sup> and <i>Alnus glutinosa</i> (scaffold N50: 0.10 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Griesmann, M. et al. Phylogenomics reveals multiple losses of nitrogen-fixing root nodule symbiosis. Science. 361, eaat1743 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d20126e594">8</a></sup>, were presented in 2017 and 2018, and the <i>B. pendula</i> genome was further anchored to fourteen chromosomes. The only published Coryloideae genomes are those of two ironwood trees from the genus <i>Ostrya</i>: <i>O</i>. <i>rehderiana</i> (scaffold N50: 2.31 Mb) and <i>O. chinensis</i> (scaffold N50: 0.81 Mb), which were reported in 2018<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang, Y. et al. Genomic effects of population collapse in a critically endangered ironwood tree Ostrya rehderiana. Nat. Commun. 9, 5449 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d20126e614">9</a></sup>. However, no genomes representing any of the other three genera in Coryloideae have been disclosed and there are no published chromosome-level genomes for this subfamily.</p><p>To enrich the available genomic resources for Betulaceae, we sequenced the whole genome of <i>Carpinus fangiana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Fig1">1</a>), a member of the most species-rich genus in Coryloideae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lancaster, R. &amp; Rix, M. 705. Carpinus fangiana. Curtis’s Botanical Magazine. 28, 103–110 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d20126e627">10</a></sup>. A total of 77.85 Gb (~200x) next-generation data and 52.19 Gb (~130x) Hi-C data were used to assemble the genome. The assembly produced a genome having a total length of 386.19 Mb, with 357.84 Mb being anchored to eight chromosomes. To our knowledge, this is the first reported chromosome-level Coryloideae genome assembly. The contig N50 and scaffold N50 were 35.32 kb and 1.91 Mb, respectively. Structural annotation of the genome revealed a total of 27,381 protein-coding genes, of which 94.36% were functionally annotated. The genome was also predicted to contain 4,440 non-coding genes based on a comprehensive annotation. This chromosome-level genome of <i>C. fangiana</i> will greatly facilitate further biological studies on Betulaceae as well as the development and commercial exploitation of the genus.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="336" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Photograph and location of the <i>C. fangiana</i> tree sampled for genome sequencing. (<b>a</b>) A photograph of a <i>C. fangiana</i> individual on Emei Mountain, Leshan, Sichuan, China. (<b>b</b>) Location of the <i>C. fangiana</i> sample used for genome sequencing.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sampling, library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Fresh leaves were collected from a wild <i>C. fangiana</i> tree in Ebian, Sichuan, China (N: 29° 1′44″; S: 102°59′30″; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Fig1">1</a>) and immediately dried over silica gel. Genomic DNA was then extracted from the dried leaves using the modified Cetyltrimethylammonium Ammonium Bromide (CTAB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Doyle, J. A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochem Bull. 19, 11–15 (1987)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d20126e675">11</a></sup> method. Sequencing libraries with different insert sizes were constructed using a library construction kit (Illumina). Short paired-end libraries were constructed with insert sizes of 230, 500, and 800 bp, while the insert sizes used to construct mate pair libraries were 2, 5, 10, and 20 kb. The Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform was used to sequence 150 bp paired-end reads for all these libraries in accordance with the manufacturer’s instructions. These procedures generated a total of 115.12 Gb (~200x) raw data for <i>C. fangiana</i> genome assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 DNA sequencing metrics of <i>C. fangiana</i>, before and after quality control.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A High-through chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) library for the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome was also constructed. To this end, fresh leaves were fixed with formaldehyde to induce DNA cross-linking, after which the DNA was digested with HindIII. The resulting sticky ends were biotinylated and proximity-ligated to form chimeric junctions that were enriched for, and physically sheared into 300–700 bp fragments. These chimeric fragments were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq platform, generating 52.54 Gb (~130x) of Hi-C data (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>We also harvested six tissues (bark, branch, bract, flower, fruit, leaf) for total RNA sequencing. These samples were flash frozen in liquid nitrogen, and total RNA was extracted using the modified CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chang, S. J., Puryear, J. &amp; Cairney, J. A simple and efficient method for isolating RNA from pine trees. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 11, 113–116 (1993)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d20126e879">12</a></sup>. cDNA libraries were then constructed using the NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (NEB). The Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform was used to sequence these libraries with a read length of 2 × 150 bp, generating over 5.50 Gb raw data for each tissue (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Illumina RNA sequencing metrics, before and after quality control.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Preprocessing and genome size estimation</h3><p>Quality control checks on the raw genome data were preformed using FastQC (<a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>). Potential adapters in reads were removed using Scythe (<a href="http://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe">http://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe</a>) and low-quality reads were discarded by Sickle (<a href="http://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe">http://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe</a>). The program Lighter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Song, L., Song, L., Florea, L. &amp; Langmead, B. Lighter: fast and memory-efficient sequencing error correction without counting. Genome Biol. 15, 509 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d20126e996">13</a></sup> was then used to correct sequence errors in the remaining reads. For mate pair reads, we also used FastUniq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xu, H. et al. FastUniq: a fast de novo duplicates removal tool for paired short reads. PloS One. 7, e52249 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1000">14</a></sup> to remove duplicates. In total, 77.85 Gb, ~200x high-quality next-generation sequencing data and 52.19 Gb, ~130x high-quality Hi-C data were generated for <i>de novo</i> assembly of the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>Quality control of transcriptome data was performed using a custom Perl script. Reads were discarded if (1) the proportion of unidentified nucleotides in one read exceeded 5%, or (2) over 65% of the read’s bases had a phred quality below 8. After eliminating low-quality reads, the quantity of retained data for each tissue was above 5.50 Gb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab2">2</a>). The RNA-seq reads were then assembled using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1020">15</a></sup>. CD-Hit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, W. Z., Jaroszewski, L. &amp; Godzik, A. Tolerating some redundancy significantly speeds up clustering of large protein databases. Bioinformatics. 18, 77–82 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1024">16</a></sup> was used to eliminate redundant transcript sequences, and candidate coding regions in the transcript sequences were identified by TransDecoder (<a href="https://transdecoder.github.io">https://transdecoder.github.io</a>).</p><p>Before genome assembly, we estimated the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome’s size by performing a combined analysis using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1038">17</a></sup> and GenomeScope<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vurture, G. W. et al. GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics. 33, 2202–2204 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1042">18</a></sup>. Reads from the short-insert libraries were first processed by Jellyfish to assess their <i>k</i>-mer distribution, using a <i>k</i> value of 17. Then, GenomeScope was used to estimate the genome size based on the <i>k</i>-mer distribution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Fig2">2</a>). The genome was thereby estimated to be around 396.74 Mb long.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="584" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>K-mer distribution used to estimate the genome’s size. The distribution was determined based on the Jellyfish analysis using a <i>k</i>-mer size of 17.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly</h3><p>Preliminary <i>de novo</i> assembly of the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome was performed with Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1084">19</a></sup>, which can effectively manage high-throughput data from heterozygous samples. Assembly using Platanus proceeded via three steps: (1) contig-assembly, in which de Bruijn graphs were constructed using the clean reads from short paired-end libraries and the sequences of contigs were then displayed in the graphs; (2) scaffolding, in which reads from all next-generation libraries (short paired-end and mate pair) were mapped to contigs, after which contigs considered to be linked were combined into scaffolds; (3) gap closing, in which reads that mapped onto scaffolds were collected to cover the gaps between them. GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience. 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1088">20</a></sup> was used to further close the gaps based on reads from all the paired-end libraries, after which the automated HaploMerger2 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Huang, S. et al. HaploMerger: reconstructing allelic relationships for polymorphic diploid genome assemblies. Genome Res. 22, 1581–1588 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1092">21</a></sup> was used to rebuild the above assembly and implement flexible and sensitive error detection. After discarding scaffolds smaller than 1 kb, a high-quality <i>de novo</i> assembled <i>C. fangiana</i> genome was obtained. The size of this genome (386.19 Mb) was 97.34% of the estimated value (396.74 Mb) and its GC content was 37.59%. The scaffold N50 and N90 values were 1.91 Mb and 0.43 Mb, while the contig N50 and N90 were 35.32 kb and 8.54 kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Summary of <i>C. fangiana</i> genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 1–11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1221">22</a></sup> program was used for quality assessment of the Hi-C data. Valid interaction pairs were mapped to and used for error correction of the contigs and scaffolds assembled based on the next-generation sequencing data. Next, the contigs and scaffolds were anchored to chromosomes using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1225">23</a></sup>. In total, 357.84 Mb of scaffolds were assembled into eight chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab4">4</a>). Finally, we obtained a high-quality chromosome-level genome with a total size of 386.25 Mb. The contig N50 and scaffold N50 values of this chromosome-level assembly were 34.85 kb and 37.11 Mb, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Summary of the assembled chromosomes in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Heterozygosity assessment and repeat annotation</h3><p>To assess the heterozygosity of the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome, we first mapped reads from the 500 bp library to the assembled genome using the BWA-MEM algorithm from the Burrows-Wheeler Aligner (BWA) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv preprint arXiv. 1303, 3997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1359">24</a></sup>. SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li, H., Bob, H., Alec, W., Tim, F. &amp; Jue, R. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics. 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1363">25</a></sup> was used to convert the mapping results to BAM format, sort them, and remove duplicates. The Picard package (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>) was used to replace read groups in the bam file. Two programs (RealignerTargetCreator and IndelRealigner) from the Genome Analysis ToolKit (GATK)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1371">26</a></sup> package were used to avoid misalignments and account for the effects of indels. The SAMtools command ‘mpileup’ was used to generate a VCF format file, and the program bcftools from the SAMtools package was used to detect single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs). Finally, based on the SNPs, the heterozygosity was calculated to be 0.38% using a custom Perl script.</p><p>Repetitive sequences and transposable elements (TEs) in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome were identified using a combined procedure incorporating <i>de novo</i> and homology-based approaches at the DNA and protein levels. Tandem repeats were annotated using Tandem Repeat Finder (TRF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1384">27</a></sup>. A repeat library for the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome was generated using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) to facilitate <i>de novo</i> annotation. RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tarailo‐Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1399">28</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) was used to identify and classify the TEs at the DNA level. We also used RepeatProteinMasker to perform a WU-BLASTX search against the TE protein database in order to identify and classify TEs at the protein level. Finally, long terminal repeats (LTR) were identified using LTR-FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1407">29</a></sup>. In total, the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome was found to contain 158.69 Mb repetitive sequences, accounting for 41.08% of its length (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab5">5</a>). As shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab5">5</a>, the most common classifications assigned to these repetitive elements were Unknown (15.97% of the assembled genome) and LTRs (14.57% of the assembled genome).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-6"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Repeat element metrics for the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/6"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene annotation</h3><p>Structural annotation of gene models was performed by applying a combination of <i>de novo</i>, homology-based, and transcriptome-based methods to the repeat-masked genome. The <i>de novo</i> approach was implemented using Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics. 24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1543">30</a></sup>, Geneid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Parra, G., Blanco, E. &amp; Guigó, R. Geneid in Drosophila. Genome Res. 10, 511–515 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1547">31</a></sup>, GeneMark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lukashin, A. V. &amp; Borodovsky, M. GeneMark.hmm: new solutions for gene finding. Bioinformatics. 26, 1107–1115 (1998)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1551">32</a></sup>, glimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1556">33</a></sup>, and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1560">34</a></sup>. For homology-based prediction, TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Gertz, E. M., Yu, Y. K., Agarwala, R., Schäffer., A. A. &amp; Altschul, S. F. Composition-based statistics and translated nucleotide searches: improving the TBLASTN module of BLAST. BMC Biol. 4, 41 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1564">35</a></sup> was used to align predicted protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Ostrya chinensis</i>, <i>Ostrya rehderiana</i> and <i>Juglans regia</i> to the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome with an E-value threshold of 1E-05. Then, GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Birney, E. et al. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1590">36</a></sup> was used to obtain accurate spliced alignments by aligning homologous sequences to matched proteins. Transcriptome-based prediction was performed with the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1594">37</a></sup>, which was used to predict protein-coding regions based on the assembled transcripts of the six different <i>C. fangiana</i> tissues. The gene models obtained from the <i>de novo</i>, homology-based, and transcriptome-based annotations were combined to form a consensus gene set using EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1605">38</a></sup>. After strict filtering, a total of 27,381 non-redundant protein-coding genes were annotated in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab6">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-7"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab6" data-test="table-caption">Table 6 Summary of predicted protein-coding genes in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/7"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Functional annotation of the predicted protein genes was performed by using BLASTP with an E-value threshold of 1E-05 to search for homologous sequences in SwissProt (<a href="http://www.gpmaw.com/html/swiss-prot.html">http://www.gpmaw.com/html/swiss-prot.html</a>), TrEMBL (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org">http://www.uniprot.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1910">39</a></sup>, and KEGG (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/</a>) protein databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. BlastKOALA and GhostKOALA: KEGG tools for functional characterization of genome and metagenome sequences. J. Mol. Biol. 428, 726–731 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1918">40</a></sup>. The program hmmscan of HMMER package (<a href="http://hmmer.org">http://hmmer.org</a>) was used to search the Pfam domains. InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mulder, N. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterPro and InterProScan: tools for protein sequence classification and comparison. Methods Mol. Biol. 396, 59–70, (Springer, 2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1927">41</a></sup> was used to annotate the protein motifs and domains, and the Blast2GO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Conesa, A., Sato, Y. &amp; Morishima, K. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics. 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1931">42</a></sup> was used to obtain Gene Ontology (GO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d20126e1935">43</a></sup> IDs for each gene based on the NCBI NR database. In total, 25,836 protein-coding genes, corresponding to 94.36% of the total predicted gene models in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome were successfully functionally annotated (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab7">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-8"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab7" data-test="table-caption">Table 7 Summary of functional annotation in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/8"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also annotated non-coding RNAs in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome. tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2029">44</a></sup> was used to detect putative transfer RNAs (tRNAs) with eukaryotic parameters, resulting in the identification of 632 tRNAs. To identify other non-coding RNAs, INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics. 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2033">45</a></sup> was used to perform searches against the Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121–D124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2037">46</a></sup> database, resulting in the identification of 936 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), 197 microRNAs (miRNAs), 117 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs), and 232 small nucleolar RNAs (snoRNAs) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab8">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-9"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab8" data-test="table-caption">Table 8 Summary of non-coding genes in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/9"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The sequencing data including the Illumina genome data (SRA accession: SRX6070999-SRX6071006), Hi-C data (SRA accession: SRX6071007), and Illumina transcriptome data (SRA accession: SRX6070994-SRX6070998, SRX6071008) were submitted to the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under BioProject accession number PRJNA548027<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title=" NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/insdc.sra:SRP201422&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2170">47</a></sup>. The assembled genome was deposited at DDJB/ENA/GenBank under accession number VIBQ00000000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Yang, X. et al. Carpinus fangiana isolate Cfa_2016G, whole genome shotgun sequencing project. Genbank, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:VIBQ00000000.1&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2174">48</a></sup>. Repeat annotations, gene model annotations and non-coding RNA annotations, the CDS sequences for the coding and non-coding genes, the protein sequences for the coding genes, as well as two custom Perl scripts were deposited at figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yang, X. Y., Wang, Z. F., Zhang, L., Hao, G. Q. &amp; Yang, Y. Z. Data and material for the Carpinus fangiana genome. figshare, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8549204.v6&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2178">49</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Assessment of the genome assembly</h3><p>We evaluated the completeness of the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome assembly in two ways. First, all the paired-end reads were mapped to the assembly genome with BWA. The aligned outputs were then analyzed using SAMtools. The mapping rate for each library was above 90% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab9">9</a>). Furthermore, the coverage of the genome after gap elimination was 99.74%, with 95.05% having at least 100x coverage. Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Loannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d20126e2200">50</a></sup> was also used to evaluate the completeness of the genome assembly. 95.30% of the “complete BUSCOs” were successfully identified in the assembly, and the proportion of “missing BUSCOs” was only 4.10% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Tab10">10</a>). These results demonstrate the high reliability and completeness of the reported genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-10"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab9" data-test="table-caption">Table 9 Mapping ratio of Illumina DNA reads for the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/10"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-11"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab10" data-test="table-caption">Table 10 Assessment of BUSCOs in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/tables/11"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Finally, we evaluated the assembly of the eight chromosomes. To this end, the anchored genome was split into ‘bins’ of 100 kb in length. The number of Hi-C read pairs covered by any two ‘bins’ was used to define the signal for the interaction between those ‘bins’, and these signal intensities were plotted in the form of a heat map. The signal intensities clearly divided the ‘bins’ into eight distinct groups, demonstrating the high quality of the chromosome assembly (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0370-5/MediaObjects/41597_2020_370_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="615" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Heat map of chromosomal interactions in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome. Cfa01-Cfa08 represent the eight chromosomes in the <i>C. fangiana</i> genome. The horizontal and vertical coordinates represent the order of each ‘bin’ on the corresponding chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0370-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Improvement of gene annotation quality</h3><p>To maximize the reliability of the gene annotation process, repeat regions in the assembled genome were masked before gene annotation. Mirroring the procedure used to filter gene annotation, EVM was initially used to merge the results obtained by <i>de novo</i>, homolog-based, and transcriptome-based predictions. Genes were then discarded if: (1) their CDS length was below 150 bp; (2) their putative coding regions could not be accurately translated into protein sequences; (3) they possessed early termination codons; or (4) they were only supported by <i>de novo</i> predictions. In addition, PASA was used to identify untranslated regions (UTRs).</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>This work relied on many software tools. The versions, settings and parameters of these tools are given below.</p>
              <p><b>(1) FastQC</b>: version 0.11.5, default parameters; <b>(2) Scythe</b>: version 0.994 BETA, parameters: -q sanger --quiet; <b>(3) Sickle</b>: version 1.33, parameters: pe -t sanger -q 20 -l 50 -n --quiet; <b>(4) Lighter</b>: version 1.0.7, parameters: -K 21 360000000; <b>(5) FastUniq</b>: version 1.1, default parameters <b>(6) Trinity</b>: trinityrnaseq-2.6.4, parameters: --seqType fq --JM 260G; <b>(6) CD-Hit</b>: version 4.6, default parameters; <b>(7) TransDecoder</b>: version 5.2.0, default parameters; <b>(8) Jellyfish</b>: version 1.1.10, parameters: count command: -m 17 -s 4G -c 7, dump command: -c -t, histo command: default parameters; <b>(9) GenomeScope</b>: version 2.0, parameters: 17 (<i>k</i>-mer length) 150 (read length); <b>(10) Platanus</b>: version 1.2.1, default parameters for the all three steps, <b>(11) GapCloser</b>: version 1.12, parameter: -l 150; <b>(12) HaploMerger2</b>: version HaploMerger2_20151124, default parameters for the followed running processes: carrying out batchA to batchE with the recommended pipeline, among which batchA was repeated 3 times and batchD was repeated 2 times, respectively; <b>(13) HiC-Pro</b>: version 2.10.0, default parameters; <b>(14) LACHESIS</b>: released in 2017, parameters: CLUSTER_MIN_RE_SITES=36 CLUSTER_MAX_LINK_DENSITY=1 CLUSTER_NONINFORMATIVE_RATIO=8 ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_TRUN=22 ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_SHREDS 22; <b>(15) BWA</b>: version 0.7.12-r1039, default parameters; <b>(16) SAMtools</b>: version 1.5, parameters: view command: -bS, sort command: -O BAM, depth command: -Q 40, mpileup command: -DSug -C 50, default parameters for the rmdup, index and flagstat commands; <b>(17) Picard</b>: version 1.80, parameters: SORT_ORDER =coordinate RGPL =illumina RGPU =illumina; <b>(18) GATK</b>: version 3.3-0-g37228af, default parameters for the two programs RealignerTargetCreator and IndelRealigner; <b>(19) bcftools</b>: version 0.1.19-44428 cd, parameters: view –Ncg; <b>(20) TRF</b>: version 4.07b, parameters: Match=2 Mismatch=7 Delta=7 PM=80 PI=10 Minscore=50 MaxPeriod=500 -d –h; <b>(21) RepeatModeler</b>: version 1.0.4, parameters: -pa 30 -database Fan; <b>(22) RepeatMasker</b>: version open-4.0.5, parameters: -pa 30 -species all -nolow -norna -no_is -gff; <b>(23) RepeatProteinMasker</b>: version 2.1, parameters: -engine abblast -noLowSimple -pvalue 1e-04; <b>(24) LTR-FINDER</b>: version 1.05, default parameters; <b>(25) Augustus</b>: version 2.5.5, parameters: --species=arabidopsis; <b>(26) Geneid</b>: version 1.4, parameters: -3 -P; <b>(27) GeneMark</b>: version 3.47, parameters: -f gff3; <b>(28) GlimmerHMM</b>: version 3.0.4, default parameters; <b>(29) SNAP</b>: version 2006-07-28, default parameters; <b>(29) GeneWise</b>: version 2.4.1, parameters: -tfor/-trev -gff; <b>(30) EVM</b>: version 1.1.1, default parameters; <b>(31) PASA</b>: version 2.0.2, parameters: for Launch_PASA_pipeline.pl step: -C -R -r–ALIGNERS blat, gmap, default parameters for the below two steps: asa_asmbls_to_training_set.extract_reference_orfs.pl and pasa_asmbls_to_training_set.dbi; <b>(32) BLASTP</b>: version 2.2.30+, parameters: -evalue 1e-5 -outfmt 7; <b>(33) Interproscan</b>: version 5.25-64.0, parameters: -dp -f tsv; <b>(34) tRNAscan-SE</b>: tRNAscan-SE-2.0, default parameters; <b>(35) BUSCO</b>: version 2.0, parameters: -m genome -c 20.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="MJM.
